# 285\n
{Oxford, Bodleian Library, Laud Misc 108, fols. 204r-219va, hand C: Havelok}\n
C14a1\n
554 300 S\n
{=W Norfolk=}\n
{=Sample represents the whole of Havelok (fols. 204r-219va). King Horn\n
(fols. 219va-228r) is also in hand C, but in mixed language - Norfolk\n
with a much more southerly ingredient. KH has not been tagged=}\n
{=Script - Textura semiquadrata=}\n
{=Status - the manuscript text has no punctuation. New sections are\n
indicated by enlarged blue initials decorated with red - as noted in the\n
tagged text. Smithers (1987) does not individually note these capitals\n
nor do his section breaks always follow those indicated by them. Tagging\n
notes and textual notes up to date=}\n
{=Note that Smithers (1987) expands abbreviations silently, except\n
where the titulus (n or m as required) is shown in italics to\n
differentiate it from hyper-metrical -en. As usual I mark all\n
manuscript abbreviations and only capitalise where the manuscript\n
has capitals or littorae notabiliores - mostly only at the beginnings\n
of lines. The shapes of thorn and <y> are occasionally rather similar, but\n
usually are distinguished, thorn has a slightly raised ascender\n
which tends to bend back and <y> is usually dotted. The shapes of\n
C and T are very similar. The common CT is usually ligatured, the\n
scribe giving the shaft of T following C a much taller stroke. When\n
not ligatured, T usually has a top stroke to the left as well as to the\n
right of the shaft. Smithers assumes the letter to be C when the left\n
part of the top stroke is missing and emends to T. But there are\n
sufficient numbers of cases where C is T-shaped and T is C-shaped for\n
the assumption that the figurae were, at least to a certain extent,\n
interchangeable. I have therefore tended to follow Smithers\'\n
\'emendations\' though have not called them emendations or put them in\n
square brackets. I have noted, however, if the letter T is unusually\n
C-like or vice-versa. The scribe tends to make his minim strokes\n
forming N, M and U separately with no clear or consistent joining at the\n
top for M and N or at the foot for U. He sometimes uses an oblique\n
hairline stroke to differentiate I, but not commonly. This means that\n
context is often the only way to decide which letter is intended. This\n
only becomes a serious problem with the word "knight" which is sometimes\n
spelled KINCT with clear hairline stroke on the first minim. This\n
matches similar CIN- spellings in Layamon and elsewhere and must be\n
treated as a genuine orthographic form whatever its phonological\n
implications may be (see Laing & Lass 2005). There are many\n
instances of the word, however where the three minims making up -NI-\n
or -IN- in this word are quite impossible to differentiate. In those\n
cases I have transcribed the word with the historically expected\n
spelling KNI- and have only transcribed KIN- where there are strong\n
positive reasons for the reading=}\n
{~f204ra~}\n

$faela/vpt13_FEL+EDE $/vpt13[L]_+EDE\n
{=Smithers (1987) and Skeat-Sisam (1956) follow Onions (who himself\n
credits Tolkien - see Philologica: the Malone Anniversary Studies\n
(1949), p. 157) in taking this to mean "terrify" or "scare" and\n
cite OI faela, faelda. MED takes the word to be from OE fe:olan\n
"approach", "have to do with". Although the letter F in MED was\n
edited very early, it was published after the Onions article.\n
Holthausen (1901) takes the word to be a form of "follow"=}\n

{=The above seems to me to make better sense of these lines than\n
previous efforts. It avoids Holthausen\'s supposition that the HEM\n
refers to enemies and that therefore some lines before must be missing.\n
Skeats\' radical emendation is rendered unnecessary, and it sticks to\n
the MS reading without invoking Smithers\' (1987) "delayed preposition".\n
The translation would be "If.. she could take control of England and do it\n
into the hands of those who were pleasing to her and could protect her\n
person...". This identifies yAR HIRE WERE Q~ME with the thousand men\n
by her side=}\n

$adread/vpp{rh}_A+DRED $a-/xp-v_A+\n
{=Smithers (1987) reads ADRAD with no editorial comment. Skeat-Sisam\n
(1956) says the reading is ADRED altered to ADRAD. The last two\n
letters are squashed together somewhat, but the penultimate is\n
certainly not A and appears to me to be E-shaped. If there has\n
been any attempt at alteration I don\'t think it is by the main\n
scribe. ADRED obviously does not provide a pure rhyme with GAD\n
which presumably explains the editorial readings. But cf. ADRED\n
in line 1259 rhyming with BED. The main scribe clearly has ADRED\n
as one possible variant and he is not overly squeamish about\n
spoiling rhymes=}\n

$bebed/viK2{rh}_BE-BEDD+E $be-/xp-v_BE- $/viK2{rh}_+E\n
{=Using the verb (< OE beddian) is a common way of saying "provide a bed\n
for" in ME. The prefixed variant is apparently unique however, and BE-\n
rather than BI- is not this scribe\'s usual spelling. Smithers (1987)\n
makes no comment on the word although he prints BEBEDDE as a single\n
form. He does not gloss it. Skeat-Smithers (1956) is worried by the lack of\n
other citations and BE- rather than BI- and suggests that the letters have\n
been erroneously repeated and that the word should be emended to BEDDE.\n
An alternative would be to take BEDDE as the noun and translate "Nor did\n
he richly for them by (in respect to) bed". However, this scribe\'s usual\n
form for "by" is also BI not BE=}\n

$thereby{p}/av-k_yE-BI $by{p}/pr-k_-BI\n
{=sic. yE is more likely to be an error for "there" (as Holthausen\n
(1901) and Skeat-Sisam (1956) assume) than an error for yEM as\n
emended by Smithers (1987). yE is quite frequently written for\n
yER in other eME hands and the supposition that it stands for\n
yEM would make this a unique example in Havelok of the \'Scandinavian\'\n
form for "them". There are a number {of examples in the text of\n
yEI/yEY for "they", but apparently only one of yERE for "their".\n
We know that "them" was the last of the set of three regularly\n
to appear in Middle English, so a form yEM in Havelok might\n
perhaps be unexpected beside so many examples of HEM. Whether\n
such forms were in the rather more northerly Lincolnshire\n
original is unknowable=}\n

$ha:wian?/vpt13_HAU+EDE $/vpt13_+EDE\n
$/P13NM_HE\n
$e:ode/vpt13_YEDE\n
{=This is the worst problem of Smither\'s (1987) locus desperatus and the\n
Cambridge text is no longer running so supplies no help. Skeat-Sisam\n
(1956) emend heavily and frankly unjustifiably, to make a sensible\n
reading text but which cannot be arrived at from the MS readings\n
and is word substitution purely. The last two words are clearly\n
separated and I agree with the Skeat-Sisam reading HE YEDE\n
(retained by them in favour of emendation earlier in the line),\n
pace Smithers who insists on HEyEDE or HE yEDE, denying the\n
possibility of HE YEDE. The problem letter is \'y\'-shaped rather\n
than thorn-shaped, though undotted. It could ceteris paribus be\n
read as either Y or y - there is a cline of shapes and dotting\n
is not consistent. But HE yEDE makes no sense and Smithers\'\n
suggestion that the text should read HEyEDE from OE ae:Yan\n
"to conjure on oath, to exhort" needs a lot of special pleading. The\n
final E of the supposed past participle Smithers admits would be\n
\'an error\' (at least in this form of language) while a rhyme of an\n
unstressed final syllable on a (supplied) main verb - LEDE in the next\n
line would be unprecedented in the poem. My alternative suggestion is\n
that HAUEDE is not from "have" (thus obviating the necessity of\n
finding a dependent past participle) but is from OE ha:wian\n
"to regard, view, look on". (See MED s.v. hauen). We would\n
perhaps expect this scribe to spell the word *HAWEDE, especially\n
because of the ambiguity with "have", but there is a certain\n
amount of U W variation - for a parallel example see line 1402\n
SHEUE "show" (rhyming with KNEWE) and a reverse example LOWERD\n
"lord" in line 622. And/or, with the other signs of textual\n
corruption, the present scribe may not have understood the\n
word. But if we accept OE ha:wian, we would then have something\n
syntactically simple: "When the traitor was watching him he went\n
(so) that (i.e. "as if") he should lead him forth and drown him in\n
the sea". This would make the point that Godard saw him bind and gag\n
Havelok and make as if he were going to do the deed (which in the end\n
of course he didn\'t) but it brings more life to the story that Godard\n
had every confidence that the deed was about to be done and assumed\n
it then was=}\n
{\\}\n

$whale/nOd{rh}_HWEL\n
{=Smithers (1987) doesn\'t like the repetition of "whale" with a different\n
spelling. Nevertheless it seems to me that the present scribe\n
probably thought "whale" was intended in each case, with different\n
rhyme words justifying the different spellings. The original text\n
is perhaps less likely to have intended two whales, but if sorting\n
out the original is deemed necessary I\'d prefer the Skeat-Sisam\n
(1956) solution to Smithers\' one: HWEL is a "correction" (to give a word\n
for "whale") of HEL which was intended for "eel". At least "eel" was\n
a common fishing staple unlike Smithers\' putative Dutch "kwal"\n
"jellyfish" to explain QUAL=}\n
{\\}\n
$/P13NM_*HE\n

{=sic=}\n
$both/pnOd{rh}_BAyE\n
{=i.e. "take me with thee to Denmark both [of us together]". This\n
slightly corrupt line has caused problems. Skeat-Sisam (1956) have one\n
solution - to read NIMEN WIT for NIM IN WITL taking WIT to be the\n
only example in this text of the dual. I think this unlikely (though\n
compare Genesis and Exodus of similar date and provenance which uses\n
the dual a number of times). But NIM is very clearly followed by a\n
space and then three minims and WIT clearly has an ascender after it.\n
Smithers\' (1987) solution seems even more fanciful and clumsy however.\n
He reads NIM IN WIT LIyE and gives two possible interpretations -\n
"let us take our journey" - emending NIM IN to NIME, but prefers,\n
on the grounds of palaeographical likelihood, NIM IN ... TO "go to"\n
and WIT LIyE "with a body of men". My solution seems to me simpler\n
and more likely. The scribe often fails to finish words and the\n
three minims of "in" can equally well be read as M. The putative L\n
at the end of WIT is identical to an ascender of H and nothing else\n
need be supposed. The phrase "take me with thee" would then pick up\n
lines 1340-1 below "For I shall never be happy until I\'ve seen\n
Denmark with my eyes"=}\n
{\\}\n

$/TOd_yE\n
$kitthen?/vnOd_KIyy+ING $/vnOd_+ING\n
$deal/vpp{rh}_DEL+ED $/vpp[L]{rh}_+ED\n
{=This is a textual difficulty as the reading is uncertain and those\n
suggested do not correspond to known words. Skeat reads yE KILyING\n
and then simply emends to ILK yING. Smithers (1987) rightly\n
rejects such a violent emendation which makes no accounting of\n
the corruption. He also reads KILyING and evokes ON ky\'la "fill\n
one\'s belly with" and ky\'la o,l "tipple ale" and translates\n
"?liquor for tippling". MED reads KIWING and gives no suggestion\n
as to the reading. I don\'t see the reading as either KILyING or\n
KIWING. The difficulty is not the fourth letter which is clearly\n
thorn (ruling out KIWING). Even if it be read as a Y or as a wynn\n
it doesn\'t help us with the third letter. Its shape is not that of\n
L which this scribe makes with a strong upright ascender (leaning\n
forwards if anything rather than backwards and usually (though not\n
always) with a leading fork or splaying at the top and a slight\n
rightward hook at the foot. This stroke is backward-sloping and\n
is parallel to, and most like, the first stroke of the immediately\n
following thorn. I think the letters can only be read as a double\n
thorn. There is a common early Middle English scribal habit, with\n
double letters that have ascenders, of representing the first of the\n
two by the ascender only, the lobe or limb following the second\n
ascender standing for both lobes. This is most commonly seen with B in\n
e.g. the forms of the verb "to have" where HABBE looks like HALBE\n
(cf. *UBBE below, fol. 215va and line 2289 of Smithers\' (1987) and\n
WIKKE below, fol. 216va line 2459 of Smithers). If we read KIyyING\n
the word could be either from OE cy:Yan, meaning "acquaintanceship"\n
(cf. the use of the verbal noun with this meaning in Ancrene Riwle)\n
or more likely from the verb derived from cy:YYu - see MED s.v.\n
kitthen, where the translation could be "friendly relations, friendly\n
behaviour". The line would then be translated: "when they had shared\n
(participated in) (see MED delen sense 5) friendly behaviour"=}\n
{\\}\n

{*sj context*}\n
{=Emendation of YOU to NOU or to YOUR "of you" have both been suggested.\n
If emendation is avoided, I think YOU could be reflexive here. The\n
sense, following "come unto me" requires the lads to be the subject\n
here and so YOU cannot be the object. "Cursed be the man who may flee\n
(from) you" (which seems to be Smithers\' reading) would be technically\n
possible but formally and semantically odd. Whereas "Cursed be any of\n
you who hence flee" seems to fit the case and could just about be\n
encompassed by the syntax suggested, literally: "Cursed be (ye) who\n
yourselves hence flee=}\n

$waergan/vpp-pl{rh}_WEREW+ED $/vpp[W]-pl{rh}_+ED\n
{=This is taken by all editors so far to be the same word as WIRWED, six lines\n
below, from OE wirgan, wyrgan meaning "strangled, throttled". This seems to\n
me to be formally and semantically far less likely than that WEREWED is from\n
OE wiergan, waergan "to curse". (Note also that Smithers (1987) has\n
WEREWED correctly in the text but cites the form as WERWED in his\n
glossary). WIRWED in line 1922 is, however, doubtless from wyrgan and\n
has the <i>-spelling expected in the language of both the original of\n
Havelok and of the copying scribe (Lincs and W. Norfolk respectively).\n
The <e>-spelling in WEREWED might have been adopted as a form of wyrgan\n
for the sake of rhyme, but the sense "cursed" from wiergan, waergan\n
actually fits much better in the context of the verse: "A curse on who\n
feels pity - for they deserved it. What were they doing there (i.e. they\n
should never have been there), they were accursed (damned)" (i.e. they\n
had put themselves beyond redemption (or pity) by their own actions -\n
cf. the common phrase "wereged gastes"). Note also that Vices and\n
Virtues (Essex) has virtually the same form for the plural past\n
participle of waergan - wEREwEDE=}\n
{\\}\n
$so/av_*SO\n
$long/av_LONGE\n
$have/vpt23_HAU+EDEn $/vpt23_+EDEn\n

$/P13GM_IS\n
$weald/nOd{rh}_WOLD\n
{=Editors have been able to make little of this. Skeat-Sisam (1956)\n
drastically rewrites to HWAT yIS BARET OWETH ON WOLD - "what\n
this strife signifies". Smithers (1987) is rightly cautious and\n
leaves the line unemended with the comment that the use of WOLD with\n
the verb "to be" is like ON valdr with verb "to be", meaning\n
"is responsible for". He gives no suggested translation and admits\n
that the line remains an enigma, so I\'m not sure that this takes\n
us any farther forward. My tentative suggestion is to take\n
(ON)-HWAT as the verb which is the simplest solution syntactically.\n
Since a present tense is required it cannot be a form of cweYan\n
- although HWAT appears twice for the pret sg of cweYan elsewhere\n
in the poem. It could only be a reverse spelling HW for W for the\n
verb witan "know, be aware of, show respect to". There is no OE evidence\n
for a verb onwitan, so one would have to assume that the Havelok\n
poet\'s predilection for separable prefix verbs allowed it - if so\n
that might have accounted for the subsequent corruption. It\'s not\n
very satisfactory but would give a sensible reading with little or\n
no need for emendation: "better it is that I go myself to see that\n
this strife (i.e. those involved in it) knows about (acknowledges)\n
his authority (significance, power)"}\n
{\\}\n

{=sic - as Skeat rightly reads, though he \'regularises\' to MOUHTE.\n

ligatured, the second stroke of C (which is clearly C-shaped not\n
T-shaped) combines as the cross stroke of T. The shaft of T is\n
exaggeratedly long, but it has no sign of the splaying or forking\n
at the top that is always visible on H, and no sign of any second\n
separate stroke forming a limb to indicate H=}\n

$crask?/aj-av_CARSC\n
{=Or read CARST, TARST. Editors explain the form as a reduction of AT ARST\n
or TO ARST. I wonder whether it might originally have been a\n
corruption of an adverbial form from Scandinavian karskr - cf.\n
OI karskr, kaskr "swift, vigorous, strong". The editorial explanation\n
is a formal and textual anomaly. Whatever one may think of the formal\n
likelihood of TARST from TO ARST or AT ARST, semantically it seems\n
very unlikely. Godrich has already been locked in fierce combat with\n
Ubbe from morning till night, so can hardly be said "then first to\n
go against the Danish", whereas "fiercely, strongly" is exactly right.\n
See MED s.v. crask for variant spellings such as "crast" and "karsk"\n
making it logically feasible to extend the range to include e.g. the\n
spellings CARSC, CARST=}\n

{=sic - the illuminated capital is not like either of the illuminator\'s\n
usual forms for *Q - one with a tail extending horizontally to the right\n
from the floor of the body of the letter, the other with a long tail extending\n
downwards to the left of the body of the letter. This is an *O-shaped\n
letter with an element curling into the centre of it at the right hand\n
side - identical to the illuminated capital *Gs elsewhere. It is a one-line\n
blue initial *G decorated with red. No guide letter is visible. It is\n
not as large as the usual illuminated initials and it looks rather\n
squashed into the line space, so its presence may have been an afterthought.\n
